- **Failure scenario:** During liquidation of 12 positions, trader_service hiccups after position 4: cancel_all already removed every stop order, closes 5-12 fail with timeouts. The account is left holding 8 positions with zero protective orders overnight; a gap against any of them is unbounded until a human notices the red lines in scrollback.

- **Suggested fix:** Cancel protective orders per-position immediately before each close (not globally up front), abort the batch on the first close failure, and print a prominent summary of positions left unprotected (or re-place their stops on failure).

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: _handle_close_all (mmr_cli.py:2811+) calls mmr.cancel_all() (account-wide, removing every stop/trailing-stop), only warns on cancel_all failure, then loops closes and merely prints red on each failure with no abort, rollback, or re-protection — contrast with execute_resize_plan which re-creates protectives. A mid-liquidation trader_service hiccup genuinely leaves positions naked overnight with unbounded gap risk until a human reads scrollback. Real capital-safety fragility. Fix: cancel protectives per-position immediately before each close and abort/summarize on first failure.


### [x] HIGH — approve() marks the proposal FAILED on any client-side exception (including the 30s RPC timeout) even though the server may still place live orders — and no expiry mechanism ever prunes stale PENDING proposals

- **Location:** `trader/sdk.py:1426`  ·  _reviewer: execution_

- **Mechanism:** sdk.approve() calls place_expressive_order over RPC with the MMR default 30s timeout. Server-side, place_expressive_order awaits whatIfOrderAsync and multiple leg placements with NO timeouts of its own; if the gateway is slow the client raises TimeoutError, the except block transitions the proposal to FAILED (a terminal, immutable state), and returns failure — while the server continues and transmits the orders seconds later. There is no reconciliation: order_ids are only recorded on the success path, so the live position is orphaned from its proposal. Separately, ProposalStatus.EXPIRED exists in the state machine but nothing in the codebase ever transitions a proposal to it, and approve() performs no staleness check — a PENDING proposal with week-old limit/stop prices is executable as-is (only amount→quantity is recomputed at approve time; limit_price/stop_loss_price are not revalidated against the current market).

- **Failure scenario:** Gateway is reconnecting; approve 42 times out client-side after 30s and prints FAILED. The server completes the bracket 10s later — live position, live stops, proposal says FAILED with no order_ids. The operator (or LLM loop, seeing FAILED) re-proposes and re-approves the same trade, doubling the position. Alternatively: a 6-day-old PENDING 'BUY limit 100 / SL 95' proposal is approved after the stock fell to 80 — the limit fills immediately and the stop at 95 (above market) triggers an instant stop-out loss.

- **Suggested fix:** Give the approve RPC a generous explicit timeout and, on timeout, leave the proposal APPROVED and reconcile against open orders (by orderRef='proposal') instead of forcing FAILED; add a max-age check (auto-EXPIRE) and re-validate spec prices against a fresh snapshot at approve time.

- **Materiality:** Both halves verified. approve() (sdk.py:1424-1426) forces the proposal to terminal FAILED on ANY exception including the 30s RPC TimeoutError, while place_expressive_order keeps running server-side and can transmit the bracket seconds later — order_ids only recorded on the success path, so a live position is orphaned from a FAILED proposal and the operator/LLM re-proposes → doubled position on real money. Also confirmed nothing in the codebase ever transitions to EXPIRED and approve() does no staleness/price revalidation, so a week-old limit/stop is executable at a now-wrong price. Real, concrete capital-loss scenarios for exactly this system. Leave APPROVED on timeout + reconcile by orderRef, and add auto-EXPIRE/price revalidation.


### [ ] HIGH — SELL proposals are sized and executed with no awareness of the current position — accidental naked shorts

- **Location:** `trader/sdk.py:1156`  ·  _reviewer: wrong-thing_

- **Mechanism:** Neither the propose path nor the approve/execute path ever looks at the existing position for the symbol. PositionSizer.compute (called at sdk.py:1156) takes no action/position parameter — a SELL is auto-sized to the same fresh base_position_usd as a BUY. approve (sdk.py:1372-1400) converts amount to qty from the snapshot and place_expressive_order (trading_runtime.py:848) places the SELL with no position check; RiskGate checks only instrument filter/open orders. get_shortable_shares exists on the Trader (trading_runtime.py:1483) but is never consulted on this path.

- **Failure scenario:** LLM holds $1,200 of AAPL and proposes `SELL AAPL --confidence 0.6` intending to trim. Auto-sizer allocates base $5,000; approve computes ~24 shares at $210 and sells them: the $1,200 long becomes a ~$3,800 naked short, with margin/borrow costs and inverted exposure. Same mechanism silently opens a short for a symbol not held at all.

- **Suggested fix:** At propose and again at approve, fetch the current position: cap 'SELL to reduce' at held quantity by default and require an explicit --short/--allow-flip flag (plus a shortable-shares check) to go net short.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed the mechanism: PositionSizer.compute() takes no action/position parameter (only confidence/portfolio_state/price/liquidity/volatility), and neither propose nor approve fetches the current position for the symbol — approve just converts a base-sized USD amount to qty and places the SELL. So a SELL is auto-sized identically to a BUY, and get_shortable_shares (which exists on Trader) is never consulted on this path. For a live-money LLM loop this is a serious capital-safety defect: an intended 'trim' can flip a modest long into a larger naked short with borrow/margin cost and inverted exposure, or open a short in a symbol not held at all. Capping SELL-to-reduce at held quantity and gating net-short behind an explicit flag + shortable check is the correct guard.


### [x] HIGH — Live strategy dispatch feeds unbounded raw-tick pseudo-bars to on_prices — configured bar_size and downloaded history are ignored, so live behavior diverges completely from backtest

- **Location:** `trader/strategy/strategy_runtime.py:243`  ·  _reviewer: architecture_

- **Mechanism:** on_ticker_next converts each live Ticker into a one-row frame where open=high=low=close=last-or-mid (normalize_ticker) and pd.concat's it onto self.streams[conId] forever, then calls strategy.on_prices(streams[conId]). Three consequences: (1) the strategy's configured bar_size ('1 min' etc.) is never applied — rolling(20) means 20 ticks (seconds), not 20 bars, so any strategy validated by the backtester (which replays real OHLCV bars from DuckDB) produces entirely different signals live; (2) the historical bars painstakingly fetched by _fetch_history_with_resume go to DuckDB but are never seeded into streams, so after every strategy_service restart indicators warm up from zero live ticks; (3) pd.concat per tick is O(N) copy → quadratic CPU and unbounded memory across a session for every subscribed conId. The backtest/live symmetry the whole Explore→Backtest→Deploy workflow promises does not exist at the dispatch layer.

- **Failure scenario:** SMICrossOver backtests profitably on 1-min bars and is deployed. Live, it receives tick-level pseudo-bars starting from an empty frame: its 20-period indicators span ~20 seconds of ticks, generating crossover signals the backtest never saw; meanwhile streams for a 40-instrument portfolio universe grow without bound until the 24GB container is OOM-killed mid-session.

- **Suggested fix:** Resample the tick stream into bar_size bars (or subscribe to IB 1-min bars), seed streams from the DuckDB history that was just downloaded, and cap the in-memory window to what strategies need. Until then, treat live signals from any backtested strategy as unvalidated.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed at the code level: normalize_ticker builds a one-row OHLC=last frame and on_ticker_next does pd.concat per tick then calls strategy.on_prices(streams[conId]) with no resampling to the configured bar_size and no seeding from the DuckDB history that _fetch_history_with_resume just downloaded. So a strategy validated on 1-min bars runs live on unbounded raw ticks starting from empty — the backtest/live symmetry the whole workflow advertises does not exist, and the per-tick concat is O(N) → unbounded memory/CPU across a session (real OOM risk on a 40-instrument universe). This is the core correctness defect of the live path. The one thing that keeps it from being an immediate capital catastrophe is that nothing auto-executes those signals (see index 28) — but the divergence and the memory leak are both real and material.


### [x] HIGH — approve/reject place live orders with no authentication and no CSRF protection

- **Location:** `web/app.py:222`  ·  _reviewer: web_

- **Mechanism:** The POST /proposals/{pid}/approve endpoint calls m.approve(pid) which resolves the contract and submits a REAL order via trader_service; /proposals/{pid}/reject mutates proposal state. Neither endpoint requires any authentication, session, or CSRF token — they are plain HTML form POSTs. The app binds 0.0.0.0:7424 (main(), line 257) and is mapped to host 127.0.0.1:7424. Because there is no CSRF token and no auth, a cross-origin simple form POST (which browsers allow without preflight) from any website the operator visits can drive the approve endpoint. Proposal ids are small sequential integers, so an attacker can brute-force PENDING ids 1..N.

- **Failure scenario:** Operator has the dashboard reachable at 127.0.0.1:7424 and browses a malicious page in another tab. That page auto-submits <form action=http://127.0.0.1:7424/proposals/5/approve method=post> for ids 1..50. Any PENDING proposal gets approved and a live order is placed on the U26774889 live account, with no confirmation the operator ever saw.

- **Suggested fix:** Require authentication (even a shared secret / localhost-only token) and add a per-session CSRF token to the approve/reject forms validated server-side. At minimum require a same-origin/Referer check and non-guessable action tokens per proposal.

- **Materiality:** Verified: /proposals/{pid}/approve calls m.approve(pid) which places a live order, with no auth and no CSRF token, on an app that binds 0.0.0.0 (mapped to 127.0.0.1). A malicious page the operator visits can auto-POST a cross-origin form to 127.0.0.1:7424 and brute-force small sequential PENDING ids — the classic CSRF-against-localhost attack, here resulting in real orders on a live account. Bounded (attacker can only approve proposals the operator already created, not craft arbitrary trades), but approving an un-reviewed pending order is still capital risk. A localhost token + CSRF/Referer check is warranted.


### [ ] MEDIUM — Loop re-proposes the same symbol every cycle — no dedupe against existing PENDING proposals in prompt or code

- **Location:** `skills/mmr-loop-skill/scripts/loop_engine.py:302`  ·  _reviewer: llm-loop_

- **Mechanism:** The PHASE 3 prompt filters ideas by 'score > 6, not already held' but never instructs the LLM to check proposals() for existing PENDING entries, and no code deduplicates. The preset rotation revisits the same scan every len(scan_presets) cycles (and momentum/breakout frequently surface the same leader), so an unattended loop accumulates near-identical PENDING proposals for the same symbol — e.g. one AAPL BUY proposal every ~10 minutes overnight. The CLI exposes 'approve --all' ('Execute all pending proposals'), making bulk approval of the accumulated duplicates a one-command mistake; there is also no expiry sweep invoked by the loop, so stale proposals sized off hours-old prices remain approvable.

- **Failure scenario:** The loop runs unattended overnight with scan_interval=120s; NVDA tops the momentum scan repeatedly and, since it is not held, gets proposed in cycles 3, 8, 13, ... — 14 PENDING NVDA BUY proposals by morning, each auto-sized ~$10k. The user (or LLM) runs 'approve --all' to clear the queue and buys ~$140k of NVDA at the open instead of $10k.

- **Suggested fix:** Add 'check proposals() and skip symbols with an existing PENDING proposal' to the PHASE 3 prompt, and enforce it in code via a propose wrapper that queries the proposal store for a PENDING same-symbol/same-action entry before creating a new one; expire PENDING proposals older than N cycles.

- **Materiality:** Verified the PHASE 3 prompt filters only 'not already held' and never checks existing PENDING proposals, with no code dedupe, and preset rotation revisits the same leaders. An unattended overnight loop genuinely can accumulate many near-identical PENDING proposals, and 'approve --all' turns that into a one-command concentration mistake. Real for the autonomous-loop use case. Partially mitigated by the risk gate's position-size/concentration checks, but the pile-up-then-bulk-approve path is plausible and cheap to prevent (skip symbols with an open PENDING same-side proposal).


### [ ] MEDIUM — Copy-on-first-run config model silently strands existing installs on stale configs

- **Location:** `trader/container.py:54`  ·  _reviewer: ops_

- **Mechanism:** ensure_config_dir(), start_mmr.sh (cp -n), and docker-entrypoint.sh (cp -n) all copy config_defaults/*.yaml to ~/.config/mmr only when a file is missing; there is no versioning, migration, or drift detection. Any job/key added to the templates after first install (the web_dashboard and data_refresh_* pycron entries, new position_sizing limits, new trading_filters keys, the require_proposal_approval gate in trader.yaml) never reaches a live system — and there is no warning that the live config lags the shipped one. Worse, code assumes new keys exist: pycron/pycron.py main() does config['port'] / config['health_check_eval'] / config['env'] with raw indexing (lines 454-456), so a user pycron.yaml predating any of those keys makes pycron die with a bare KeyError at startup.

- **Failure scenario:** User upgrades the repo expecting the new require_proposal_approval hard gate or updated risk limits to apply to their live account; ~/.config/mmr/*.yaml is untouched, the gate stays off, and the LLM loop keeps placing direct buy/sell RPCs — the operator believes a safety control is active that is not.

- **Suggested fix:** On startup, diff live configs against bundled defaults (key-level) and warn loudly about missing keys; use .get() with defaults in pycron main(); consider a config version field with an explicit migrate command.

- **Materiality:** Verified: pycron main() uses raw config['port']/config['health_check_eval']/config['env'] indexing, and config templates are copy-on-first-run (cp -n) with no drift detection. The KeyError-on-old-config half is real but self-limiting (a broken pycron fails loudly at startup, not silently). The materially dangerous half is the silent config drift: a newly-shipped safety gate like require_proposal_approval or tightened risk limits never reaches an already-installed live system, and if the code reads it via .get(default=off) the operator believes a control is active when it is not. For a live-money single-operator box that is a genuine capital-safety gap, not enterprise hand-wringing. Key-level diff-and-warn on startup is cheap and worth it.


### [ ] MEDIUM — execute_atomic sleeps up to ~45s holding the per-DB lock, synchronously blocking the trader_service asyncio event loop on the order path

- **Location:** `trader/data/duckdb_store.py:111`  ·  _reviewer: stores_

- **Mechanism:** The retry loop in `execute_atomic` does `time.sleep(...)` (up to 32 attempts, ~45s total) while holding `self._lock`. All EventStore/ProposalStore/UniverseAccessor calls are synchronous, and several run directly on trader_service's event loop: `risk_gate.evaluate → event_store.count_since` inside `place_expressive_order` (trading_runtime.py:939) and `_log_event → event_store.append` inside `subscribe_place_order_direct`. Under cross-process file-lock contention on mmr.duckdb (CLI commands, web dashboard, backtest persist bursts), the entire event loop — IB callback dispatch via run_coroutine_threadsafe, ZMQ RPC serving, ticker publishing — stalls for up to 45s, and every other DB caller in the process queues behind the held per-DB lock.

- **Failure scenario:** While a burst of backtest subprocesses holds the mmr.duckdb file lock, an `approve` RPC reaches the risk gate; count_since retries for 45s on the loop thread. During that window trader_service processes no IB events (fills, order status), answers no RPCs (CLI status/portfolio time out), and the RPC client may time out — which combined with the executioner finding leaves an order in placed-but-reported-failed limbo.

- **Suggested fix:** Run store calls in a worker thread (asyncio.to_thread) from async paths, and give the trader_service hot path a much shorter retry budget (e.g. 2-3s) than the backtest-persist path — a risk-gate read that can't get the DB in 3s should fail loudly, not freeze the trading loop.

- **Materiality:** Verified: the retry backoff (time.sleep, up to 32 attempts) runs inside 'with self._lock' (duckdb_store.py:88), and it's a synchronous sleep. Risk-gate reads and event appends execute on trader_service's event loop thread, so under cross-process file-lock contention (backtest-persist bursts, web dashboard, CLI) the loop can stall — starving IB fill/order-status dispatch and RPC serving. Full 45s starvation is a worst case, but even multi-second freezes during fills are unacceptable on a live order path and compound finding 3's order-limbo. The 45s budget was deliberately tuned for backtest subprocesses; the fix is right — to_thread the store calls on async paths and give the trading hot path a short (2-3s) fail-loud budget rather than sharing the sweep budget.


### [ ] MEDIUM — Universes are whole dill blobs updated via unguarded read-modify-write — concurrent writers silently lose each other's updates

- **Location:** `trader/data/universe.py:211`  ·  _reviewer: stores_

- **Mechanism:** `UniverseAccessor.update()` replaces the entire universe blob in the object_store with no versioning, and every mutation path (insert(), update_from_csv_str(), trading_runtime.update_portfolio_universe, clear_portfolio_universe, CLI `universe add`) is get → mutate-in-memory → write. The DuckDB lock only makes each individual read or write atomic, not the RMW span. In trading_runtime.update_portfolio_universe (lines 768-800) the span even contains an `await get_contract_details_async(...)` — seconds of interleaving window on the same event loop — and cross-process the window spans a CLI command vs the service.

- **Failure scenario:** Two new positions A and B fill close together at IB connect. Task A reads the portfolio universe, awaits contract details; task B (A's conId not yet persisted) reads the same universe, appends B, writes; A resumes, appends A to its stale copy and writes — B's SecurityDefinition is gone from DuckDB. strategy_service's reconcile loop reads the portfolio universe from the DB and never subscribes to B; `resolve_symbol` for B returns empty, so history refresh, risk analysis and CLI orders for that live position fail as 'not resolved'. Same outcome when a user runs `universe add portfolio X` while trader_service processes a portfolio event.

- **Suggested fix:** Do the read-merge-write inside a single execute_atomic critical section (merge by conId rather than replacing the blob), or store security definitions as rows keyed (universe, conId) so inserts are additive instead of whole-blob replacement.

- **Materiality:** Verified: UniverseAccessor.update() replaces the whole blob and every mutation is an unguarded get→mutate→write; the DuckDB lock only makes each read or write atomic, not the RMW span, and update_portfolio_universe holds an await get_contract_details_async inside that span. A lost-update at connect (two near-simultaneous fills) drops a SecurityDefinition, after which strategy_service never subscribes and resolve_symbol returns empty — directly violating the precision-over-convenience principle for a live position. Cross-process (CLI 'universe add' vs the service) is an even wider window. Concrete correctness bug, not scaling theory; merge-by-conId inside one execute_atomic critical section fixes it.


### [ ] MEDIUM — Incremental refresh has no corporate-action handling — a split leaves a fake price discontinuity between old stored bars and newly fetched adjusted bars

- **Location:** `trader/data_service.py:249`  ·  _reviewer: history-workers_

- **Mechanism:** TwelveData and Massive/Polygon return split-adjusted prices as of fetch time. The refresh pipeline only fetches missing date ranges (missing() + DELETE/INSERT upsert of just the fetched window), so bars downloaded before a split remain at pre-split prices while post-split fetches are adjusted. Nothing in the listeners, data_service, or refresh loop detects splits (only the unused polygon_listener even fetches split data) or forces a full-window refetch; the only remedy is a manual `force: true` in data_refresh.yaml. IB TRADES data is split-adjusted server-side too, so IB-sourced history has the same stale-history problem after a split.

- **Failure scenario:** A portfolio symbol does a 10:1 split. The nightly refresh appends adjusted bars ~1/10th of stored history. Backtests and sweeps see a -90% one-day move: ATR explodes (position sizing shrinks to nothing or the volatility adjustment misprices risk), momentum/mean-reversion strategies emit garbage signals in backtest, and the LLM loop's research on 'recent price history' is wrong until someone notices and force-refetches.

- **Suggested fix:** On each refresh, compare the overlap bar (e.g. last stored close vs freshly fetched close for the same date); on material mismatch, refetch the full window for that symbol. Alternatively pull split events (Polygon reference API) and invalidate history on ex-date.

- **Materiality:** Verified: incremental refresh only fetches missing date ranges and upserts just that window, with no split/corporate-action detection anywhere in the refresh loop or listeners (only the unused polygon_listener even fetches splits). Since TwelveData/Polygon/IB all return split-adjusted prices as of fetch time, a split leaves pre-split stored bars alongside adjusted new bars — a fabricated ~-90% one-day gap that poisons ATR-based position sizing, backtests/sweeps, and the LLM's 'recent price history' research until someone manually force-refetches. This is silent-wrong-data feeding real sizing and research decisions; splits are infrequent but inevitable across a portfolio, and the suggested overlap-bar close comparison is a cheap guard.


### [ ] MEDIUM — IB daily bars keyed at UTC midnight vs Massive/Polygon daily at ET midnight — corrupts cross-source merges

- **Location:** `trader/listeners/ib_history_worker.py:283`  ·  _reviewer: probe-3:Intraday session, timezone, and RTH boun_

- **Mechanism:** For '1 day' bars ib_async returns datetime.date objects; the code sets `utc=True` (line 283-284) so `pd.to_datetime(date, utc=True)` yields UTC midnight, then `tz_convert(tz_info)` gives 19:00/20:00 ET of the prior day. Written back through DuckDB (`pd.to_datetime(..., utc=True)`) that is 00:00 UTC for the session. MassiveHistoryWorker (massive_history.py:54) converts Polygon's epoch-ms — daily bars are stamped at ET midnight — to 00:00 ET = 05:00 UTC. So the same trading session is stored at 00:00 UTC (IB) vs 05:00 UTC (Massive). The DuckDB upsert deletes by symbol+[min_date,max_date]+bar_size, so if a symbol's daily history is fetched from both sources the two timestamps don't overlap and both rows survive.

- **Failure scenario:** A symbol's daily bars are first populated via `data ib` then later refreshed/backfilled via Massive (or vice versa). The store ends with two daily bars per session at 00:00 and 05:00 UTC. A daily backtest then double-counts sessions (duplicate index entries break the bar-index/precompute alignment and the equity walk). Separately, any daily strategy that converts the UTC index to ET sees every IB-sourced daily bar mislabeled to the previous calendar day (19:00 ET), shifting all day-keyed logic by one session.

- **Suggested fix:** Normalize daily-bar timestamps to a single canonical convention (e.g. session date at ET midnight) in both workers before write, and make the daily upsert dedupe by session date rather than exact timestamp.

- **Materiality:** Verified in code: IB daily bars (datetime.date -> utc=True) are stamped at the 00:00 UTC instant, while Massive daily bars are converted to ET midnight (04:00/05:00 UTC), so the same session is keyed at two different instants across the two data sources this system actually uses. The DuckDB daily upsert dedupes by symbol+date-range+bar_size, not exact timestamp, so if a symbol's daily history is populated from both IB and Massive, two rows per session survive -> duplicate index entries that break backtest bar-index/precompute alignment and the equity walk. Separately, any day-keyed logic that converts the IB-sourced UTC index to ET sees every daily bar mislabeled to the prior calendar day. This is a concrete silent-wrong-data risk matching the project's 'wrong data is worse than no data' principle, not enterprise hand-wringing. Not critical because the collision requires dual-sourcing the same symbol (US defaults to Massive, international to IB), which is plausible under 'Massive first, IB fallback' and ad-hoc data ib/data massive but is not the default single-source flow; and within a single source backtests remain internally consistent (the off-by-one is a labeling/merge issue).


### [ ] MEDIUM — RPC serve loop dies permanently on any ZMQError and handler exceptions are logged at DEBUG — a live-but-deaf trading service with no diagnostics

- **Location:** `trader/messaging/clientserver.py:453`  ·  _reviewer: messaging_

- **Mechanism:** RPCServer._serve_loop breaks out of the loop on any zmq.ZMQError (logged only at DEBUG, line 454), after which the process stays alive but never answers another RPC; nothing monitors self._serve_task, so pycron sees a healthy process. Separately, when a handler raises, line 483 logs the exception at DEBUG with no traceback (logging.debug, not logging.exception), so at the default INFO level a failing place_expressive_order leaves zero server-side evidence — the only artifact is the client's reconstructed exception. Also, if unpack of an inbound request fails (line 450, e.g. malformed frame or dill-strict rejection), the error is swallowed at line 458 and no error response is sent, so the client just times out with no reason. All three violate the fail-loudly principle for a component whose silence looks identical to a network problem.

- **Failure scenario:** A transient ZMQError breaks the serve loop at 10:02. trader_service keeps publishing ticks and holding IB state, pycron reports it running, but every approve/cancel/portfolio call from then on times out. The operator restarts IB Gateway, VNCs in, etc., because nothing in the logs (at INFO) explains that the RPC loop exited hours earlier.

- **Suggested fix:** Log serve-loop exit and handler exceptions with logging.exception at ERROR; restart the serve loop on ZMQError instead of breaking (or crash the process so pycron restarts it); send an error response for unparseable requests when client_id is known.

- **Materiality:** Verified: line 454 breaks the serve loop on any zmq.ZMQError logged only at DEBUG, and handler exceptions (line 483) log at DEBUG with no traceback. For a single-operator live-money system whose whole control surface (approve/cancel/close/portfolio) rides this RPC socket, a live-but-deaf trader_service that pycron still reports healthy is exactly the 'silence looks like a network problem' failure the project's fail-loudly principle exists to prevent. The DEBUG-level swallowing of a failing place_expressive_order is directly capital-adjacent: an order that errors server-side leaves no INFO evidence. Not enterprise hand-wringing — this is basic operational observability on the trading hot path. Fix is cheap (ERROR-level logging + restart-or-crash the loop).


### [ ] MEDIUM — CLI exits 0 on every failure — dispatch swallows all exceptions and main() swallows argparse SystemExit

- **Location:** `trader/mmr_cli.py:2198`  ·  _reviewer: cli-1_

- **Mechanism:** dispatch() catches ConnectionError/ValueError/Exception, prints a status line, and unconditionally `return True`; main() (mmr_cli.py:10062) catches SystemExit from parse_args and returns. There is no path that sets a non-zero process exit code, so failed trades, unreachable services, risk-gate rejections, failed sweeps, and even malformed command lines all exit 0. The system is explicitly designed for cron (`sweep run` in pycron) and LLM/script drivers, which conventionally use exit codes; the IB-upstream JSON error path also emits `{'error': true}` without the `success` key other JSON outputs use, so even JSON consumers keying on `success` can misread it.

- **Failure scenario:** A nightly cron entry runs `mmr sweep run nightly.yaml && notify-ok`. The manifest path is typo'd; argparse errors, main() returns, exit code is 0, and the 'ok' notification fires every night while no sweeps ever run. Similarly, an orchestration script gating a hedge on `mmr sell ... ; if [ $? -ne 0 ]` never detects the failed sell.

- **Suggested fix:** Have dispatch() return/propagate an error flag, set `sys.exit(1)` in one-shot mode on any command failure, re-raise argparse SystemExit's code, and standardise error JSON on the `success: false` shape.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: main() one-shot path (mmr_cli.py:10063) does `except SystemExit: return` and ignores dispatch()'s return value; dispatch swallows exceptions and returns True (line 2198 area). No path sets a non-zero exit code, so failed trades, unreachable services, and even argparse errors exit 0. This system is explicitly cron- and LLM/script-driven (sweep run under pycron, orchestration gating on $?), where exit codes are the standard failure signal — a typo'd manifest firing a nightly 'ok' notification while nothing runs is a realistic silent failure. Standardizing sys.exit(1) on one-shot failures and the error-JSON `success:false` shape is warranted.


### [ ] MEDIUM — Sweep freshness guard only validates daily bars, so a 1-min sweep runs happily on months-stale intraday data

- **Location:** `trader/mmr_cli.py:5493`  ·  _reviewer: cli-2_

- **Mechanism:** _freshness_check reads only storage.get_tickdata(BarSize.Days1) and flags conids whose last daily bar is older than 3 calendar days. Sweeps default to bar_size '1 min' (manifest default at line 5596), but the guard never checks the bar series the sweep actually consumes — the docstring itself admits 'having yesterday's daily bar means the 1-min feed is probably also caught up', which is an assumption, not a check (the data_refresh jobs for daily and 1-min are independent). A stale or missing 1-min series passes the guard; each child backtest then runs over start=now-365d..end=now, silently computes results on the truncated series, and persists a row whose displayed period claims it covers 'now'. Secondary: the 3-day cutoff is calendar days against dt.datetime.now(), so a Monday-after-a-holiday run false-positives on perfectly fresh data (that at least fails loudly).

- **Failure scenario:** us_top20 daily refresh runs nightly but the 1-min refresh job broke two months ago. The nightly manifest sweep passes freshness (daily bars are current), 1080 1-min backtests run on data ending two months ago, and the digest/leaderboard present 'as-of-today' Sharpe/PSR numbers computed on a stale regime. The user deploys the top run to the live account based on performance that omits the last two months of market behaviour.

- **Suggested fix:** Have _freshness_check take the sweep's bar_size and check that series (fall back to daily only for daily sweeps), and compare against exchange-calendar trading days rather than calendar days.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: _freshness_check (mmr_cli.py:5493+) reads only BarSize.Days1 and its own docstring concedes 'the 1-min feed is probably also caught up' — an assumption, not a check, while data_refresh runs daily and 1-min as independent jobs. Sweeps default to 1 min, so a broken 1-min refresh passes the guard and 1080 child backtests silently run on stale intraday data while the digest presents as-of-today Sharpe/PSR. Since sweep leaderboards drive live-account deployment decisions, silently-stale inputs are a real silent-wrong-data risk. Have the guard check the sweep's actual bar_size (the calendar-vs-trading-day cutoff is a lesser, fail-loud nit).


### [ ] MEDIUM — Relative option expiration resolves to nearest-available with no tolerance, can silently pick a wildly different expiry

- **Location:** `trader/mmr_cli.py:8225`  ·  _reviewer: probe-0:Options live-order path: contract constr_

- **Mechanism:** _resolve_expiration parses '3m'/'90d' to target days then picks the available expiration minimizing abs(exp - target) (mmr_cli.py:8224-8234) with no maximum-distance guard. If the only listed expirations straddle the target loosely, buy/sell will select whatever is numerically closest and immediately place a real order against it. The chosen date is printed to console but not confirmed, and in --json mode that print corrupts output.

- **Failure scenario:** A symbol whose non-expired expirations are, say, 7 DTE and 400 DTE. `mmr options buy SYM -e 3m -s ... -r C -q ... --market` targets 90 days; \|7-90\|=83 < \|400-90\|=310, so it silently buys the 7-DTE contract — a very different risk profile than the ~90-day option the user intended.

- **Suggested fix:** Reject (or require confirmation) when the closest available expiration differs from the requested relative target by more than a tolerance (e.g. >2 weeks), and echo the DTE so the mismatch is obvious.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: _resolve_expiration picks min abs(exp-target) with no max-distance guard (lines 8224-8234). On a live options order this can silently select a 7-DTE contract when ~90-DTE was intended — a very different risk profile. The chosen date IS printed to console (mitigant), but there's no confirmation, no DTE echo, and the print corrupts --json. A tolerance/confirmation guard is warranted for live-money order placement. Material.


### [ ] MEDIUM — No broker-truth reconciliation: proposals marked EXECUTED at submission (not fill), and ORDER_FILLED/CANCELLED/REJECTED events are never written by anything

- **Location:** `trader/sdk.py:1419`  ·  _reviewer: architecture_

- **Mechanism:** approve() sets EXECUTED (terminal, immutable) as soon as place_expressive_order returns trade handles — before any fill. EventType defines ORDER_FILLED/ORDER_CANCELLED/ORDER_REJECTED but a repo-wide search shows zero writers: the book subscribes to orderStatusEvent but nothing persists fills or rejections to the event store or back onto proposals. On trader_service restart, reqAllOpenOrdersAsync rebuilds the in-memory book, but nothing reconciles proposal state, verifies GTC protective orders still exist at IB (e.g. after the Gateway's nightly AUTO_RESTART or a manual TWS cancel), or detects that an 'EXECUTED' limit entry expired unfilled. The audit trail the risk gate and dashboard depend on records submissions only, never outcomes.

- **Failure scenario:** A GTC protective stop for a 'EXECUTED' bracket is cancelled from the TWS UI (or dropped across an IB session reset). No service notices; the position runs unprotected for days while proposals and the dashboard show a healthy bracketed trade. Separately, an EXECUTED limit-entry proposal whose order expired unfilled permanently misrepresents the book to the LLM loop.

- **Suggested fix:** Persist orderStatus/execDetails events (the hooks already exist on BookSubject), key them to proposal_id via orderRef, and add a startup + periodic reconcile that cross-checks EXECUTED proposals and expected protective orders against ib.reqAllOpenOrders/fills, flagging divergence loudly.

- **Materiality:** Verified: approve() sets EXECUTED (terminal, immutable) the moment place_expressive_order returns trade handles — i.e. at submission, before any fill — and ORDER_FILLED/CANCELLED/REJECTED enum values have zero writers in the codebase. The audit trail the risk gate and dashboard depend on therefore records intents, never outcomes. For a live-money book the unprotected-position scenario is the material one: a GTC protective stop cancelled from TWS or dropped across an IB session reset is never noticed, and the dashboard keeps showing a healthy bracket while the position runs naked. The hooks (orderStatusEvent/execDetails on BookSubject) already exist, so persisting outcomes + a periodic reconcile against reqAllOpenOrders is proportionate.


### [ ] MEDIUM — Proposals never expire and approve has no price-staleness or market-hours guard — market order at open after a gap

- **Location:** `trader/sdk.py:1366`  ·  _reviewer: wrong-thing_

- **Mechanism:** ProposalStatus.EXPIRED exists in the state machine but nothing in the codebase ever sets it (grep: only proposal_store.py transition tables reference it). A PENDING proposal is approvable indefinitely. approve (sdk.py:1366-1410) captures a propose-time snapshot into metadata (sdk.py:1214) but never compares it to the approve-time market: a MARKET entry executes at whatever the price is now, and a quantity-based proposal doesn't even re-quote. There is also no market-hours check — a MARKET order approved at night rests until the open and fills on the gap. `approve --all` (mmr_cli.py:2533-2540) executes every pending proposal in one keystroke, including week-old ones from an abandoned LLM session.

- **Failure scenario:** Monday: LLM proposes BUY XYZ MARKET, quantity 500, at $40 on breakout thesis. Proposal sits PENDING. Thursday pre-market XYZ has run to $55 on a buyout rumor (thesis dead). User runs `approve --all` to clear the queue: 500 shares fill at $55 — 37% above the analyzed price — with the original $42 take-profit/stop levels now nonsensical.

- **Suggested fix:** Add a max-age (auto-transition to EXPIRED on query/approve), and at approve compare live price to metadata['snapshot'] — refuse (or require --force) when drift exceeds a configurable percentage or the market is closed for a MARKET entry.

- **Materiality:** Verified: ProposalStatus.EXPIRED appears only in the state-machine transition tables — nothing ever sets it — so a PENDING proposal is approvable indefinitely, and approve does no live-vs-snapshot price comparison or market-hours check. `approve --all` executes every pending proposal in one keystroke. For this exact system (an LLM loop that proposes freely and a human clears the queue) the stale-market-order scenario is realistic and material: a days-old MARKET entry with a dead thesis fills on a gap far from the analyzed price, with now-nonsensical bracket levels. A max-age auto-EXPIRE plus a price-drift/market-closed guard at approve is a proportionate, high-value safety addition.


### [ ] MEDIUM — Strategy dispatch carries no instrument identity — stateful/multi-leg strategies cross-contaminate in multi-conid backtests

- **Location:** `trader/simulation/backtester.py:529`  ·  _reviewer: backtester_

- **Mechanism:** `strategy.on_bar(full_prices, state, idx)` (and the on_prices fallback) is called once per conid per timestamp, but neither hook receives the conid; the returned Signal is attributed to whichever conid's bar triggered the call. A strategy that keeps instance state cannot tell which instrument it is being asked about. This is not hypothetical: strategies/pairs_zscore.py:47-60 works around it by guessing the conid from 'which stored series grew in length', a heuristic that misattributes legs whenever one conid is missing a bar at a timestamp (halt, listing gap, differing sessions) — exactly the case the backtester's timeline merge explicitly supports ('No bar for this conid yet — keep waiting').

- **Failure scenario:** PairsZScore backtest over legs A and B where A has no bar at 10:31 (brief halt): the 10:31 call for B is matched to A by the length-growth heuristic, appending B's close to A's series. The log-ratio/z-score is computed on corrupted series thereafter, emitting spurious entry/exit signals whose P&L is recorded as real edge — and the same contaminated logic runs live via on_prices.

- **Suggested fix:** Pass the conid (or contract) into on_bar/on_prices (e.g. via a per-call context or an extra parameter with a default), and have the backtester and live runtime supply it; deprecate the length-growth guessing in pairs_zscore.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: backtester.py:520-529 loops over conids but calls strategy.on_bar(full_prices, state, idx) without passing the conid, and strategies/pairs_zscore.py:44-59 really does guess the instrument by 'which stored series grew in length', with an explicit same-length fallback branch. That heuristic misattributes a leg whenever one conid lacks a bar at a timestamp (halt/session gap) — a case the timeline merge explicitly supports. This corrupts the z-score series and produces phantom P&L in backtests and wrong signals live. Real correctness bug affecting an actual strategy in the repo, though scope is limited to stateful multi-leg strategies. Passing conid into the hook is the correct fix.


### [ ] MEDIUM — Signal-to-order path does not exist: auto_execute is dead config, published Signals carry conid=0, and there is no duplicate-signal suppression

- **Location:** `trader/strategy/strategy_runtime.py:267`  ·  _reviewer: strategy-rt_

- **Mechanism:** on_ticker_next only logs the signal, appends it to the event store, and writes it to the 'signal' messagebus topic. No component in the repo subscribes to that topic (MessageBusClient.subscribe has no callers outside tests), and the `auto_execute` flag parsed from strategy_runtime.yaml and stored in StrategyContext is never consulted anywhere — grep shows it is only set, serialized, and echoed back. So the advertised 'automated strategy execution' never places an order, silently, even with auto_execute: true. Compounding this for any future consumer: the Signal is published as the strategy returned it — conid defaults to 0 and on_ticker_next does not stamp the actual conId (only the event-store row gets it), quantity is 0, and there is no dedup, so a strategy whose condition holds across consecutive ticks emits a fresh identical signal on every tick.

- **Failure scenario:** Operator deploys a strategy with auto_execute: true expecting live execution; signals accumulate in the event store but no order is ever placed and nothing warns that the flag is inert. If someone later wires a consumer to the 'signal' topic, it receives instrument-less (conid=0), quantity-less signals repeated on every tick — duplicate orders on the first condition that persists across ticks.

- **Suggested fix:** Either implement the consumer (auto_execute -> proposal/RPC place_order via trader_client with per-strategy per-conid position/last-signal state) or reject configs with auto_execute: true at load time; stamp signal.conid = conId before persisting/publishing; add crossing-edge dedup keyed on (strategy, conid, action).

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: auto_execute is only stored/echoed (strategy.py:275, strategy_runtime.py:427), never consulted; on_ticker_next (strategy_runtime.py:266-267) only logs, appends to event_store, and writes to the 'signal' messagebus topic with no in-repo subscriber. The advertised 'automated strategy execution' is a silent no-op. For this operator the real path is manual propose/approve, so there is no wrong-trade capital risk — but deploying a strategy with auto_execute:true and getting nothing while signals pile up in the event store, with no warning, is a genuine operational trap and contradicts CLAUDE.md's headline claim. The conid=0/dedup half of the finding is speculative (no consumer exists), so this is fragility/false-advertising, not high-severity capital risk. Fix: reject auto_execute:true at load time (or implement the consumer).


### [ ] MEDIUM — Strategy signals dead-end: no subscriber to the MessageBus 'signal' topic and auto_execute is accepted but implemented nowhere

- **Location:** `trader/strategy/strategy_runtime.py:267`  ·  _reviewer: architecture_

- **Mechanism:** on_ticker_next publishes signals to the MessageBus 'signal' topic, but a repo-wide search finds no component that subscribes to it and no code path that turns a Signal into an order. The auto_execute flag is parsed from strategy_runtime.yaml, stored on StrategyContext, round-tripped through StrategyConfig — and never read by any execution logic. So `auto_execute: true` silently does nothing, while the CLI/docs surface it as a real knob.

- **Failure scenario:** An operator deploys a validated strategy with auto_execute: true expecting it to trade paper. Signals are written to the event store and published into the void; no orders are ever placed. Days of expected trading silently never happen, and nothing warns that the flag is inert.

- **Suggested fix:** Either implement the consumer (a signal→proposal bridge on trader_service is the natural fit for the propose/approve pipeline) or reject configs containing auto_execute with a clear 'not implemented' error — fail loudly rather than accepting a no-op safety-relevant flag.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: no component subscribes to the MessageBus 'signal' topic, and auto_execute is parsed from YAML and round-tripped through StrategyContext/StrategyConfig but never read by any execution path. A safety-relevant knob that silently does nothing directly violates this project's stated fail-loudly principle — an operator can deploy `auto_execute: true` and believe paper/live trading is happening while signals publish into the void. The right fix is small: either build the signal→proposal bridge or reject configs containing auto_execute with a 'not implemented' error. This finding also usefully bounds the blast radius of 25/26.


### [x] MEDIUM — sanity_check_order is a no-op that always returns True — no price/marketability validation before live orders

There is therefore no fat-finger guard, no limit-vs-market sanity band, and no marketability check anywhere in the order path. - **Materiality:** Confirmed: OrderValidator.sanity_check_order is a literal `return True` (line 35), yet executioner's SANITY_CHECK branch pays for a get_snapshot round-trip and calls it (executioner.py:152-162). For a live-money path this is a real fat-finger/marketability gap — a mistyped limit or a market order on a wide-spread illiquid option has no pre-trade price protection. The misleading appearance of validation makes it worse. Material.


### [ ] MEDIUM — Risk report mixes per-position market values with base-currency net liquidation without FX normalization

- **Location:** `trader/trading/portfolio_risk.py:72`  ·  _reviewer: risk-math_

- **Mechanism:** analyze() divides each position's marketValue (taken verbatim from ib_async PortfolioItem via get_portfolio_summary -> sdk.portfolio(), with no currency field or conversion anywhere in the chain) by NetLiquidation, which IB reports in the account base currency. IB's updatePortfolio values for non-base-currency instruments are denominated in the instrument's currency, so for a USD-base account holding ASX/AUD positions (a documented, actively used flow), weights, gross/net/long/short exposure, HHI and group-budget percentages for those positions are all scaled by the raw AUD number instead of its USD equivalent (~1.5x overstatement at current rates). Nothing in the pipeline carries or applies an FX rate, so even if IB semantics differ by field, the design has no place to be correct for mixed-currency books.

- **Failure scenario:** USD account with A$100k of BHP (≈US$66k) and US$400k of US stock, net liq US$500k: BHP weight reported as 20% instead of ~13%, tripping the >15% critical concentration warning and inflating the 'mining' group budget usage — the LLM redirects or rejects otherwise-fine trades (or, in the inverse FX case, misses a real breach).

- **Suggested fix:** Carry contract.currency through PortfolioSummary/sdk.portfolio(), and convert marketValue to base currency using IB's $LEDGER ExchangeRate values (already parsed in trader_service_api.get_account_cash_by_currency) before computing weights.

- **Materiality:** Verified: portfolio_risk.py:72 uses marketValue verbatim with no currency field or FX conversion anywhere in the chain, dividing by base-currency NetLiquidation. IB's PortfolioItem.marketValue is denominated in the instrument's currency, and this operator actively runs mixed-currency books (USD base + ASX/AUD is a documented flow, and live_account_setup notes AU exposure). So AUD position weights, HHI, exposure and group-budget percentages are inflated ~1.5x, tripping false concentration/budget warnings that make the LLM redirect or reject sound trades (or miss a real breach in the inverse case). It's advisory rather than order-placement, hence medium not high, but it's genuine silent-wrong-data for a book this user actually holds, and the fix (thread contract.currency + apply $LEDGER ExchangeRate already parsed elsewhere) is bounded.


### [x] MEDIUM — Group-allocation analysis swallows all exceptions, hiding budget breaches

- **Location:** `trader/trading/portfolio_risk.py:179`  ·  _reviewer: probe-2:position_groups store and group-budget e_

- **Mechanism:** The entire group-budget block is wrapped in `try/except Exception: pass` (portfolio_risk.py:147-180). Any failure querying the group store (DuckDB IOException under concurrent access, schema drift, etc.) causes group_allocations and over-budget warnings to be silently omitted from the report, with no warning surfaced to the caller. This violates the fail-loudly principle for a risk-limit check: the LLM/operator reviewing `portfolio-risk` before approving sees an empty group section and cannot distinguish 'no groups over budget' from 'group analysis crashed'.

- **Failure scenario:** Under a burst of concurrent DuckDB writes (sweep/backtest running), group_store.list_groups() raises IOException. portfolio-risk returns a report with group_allocations=[] and no warnings. Operator approves a trade that pushes the mining group well over its budget, believing the risk report cleared it.

- **Suggested fix:** Catch narrowly and attach an error/warning entry to the report (e.g. a 'group analysis unavailable' warning) instead of silently swallowing, so callers know the group check did not run.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: the entire group-budget block is wrapped in `try/except Exception: pass` (line 179-180). A DuckDB IOException (plausible under the documented concurrent-write bursts) silently drops group_allocations and over-budget warnings, leaving the operator unable to distinguish 'no breach' from 'check crashed'. Direct fail-loudly violation on a risk display. Budgets are soft limits so impact is bounded, but attaching an 'analysis unavailable' warning is a clear material fix.


### [ ] MEDIUM — Crash between bracket legs leaves staged transmit=False orders at the Gateway with no restart reconciliation

- **Location:** `trader/trading/trading_runtime.py:980`  ·  _reviewer: wrong-thing_

- **Mechanism:** The bracket's all-or-nothing guarantee (entry transmit=False -> TP transmit=False -> SL transmit=True, with rollback on failure returns) only covers graceful failures. If trader_service crashes/OOMs or the Gateway connection drops after the entry (line 980-982) or TP has been staged but before the SL transmits, the untransmitted orders remain held in IB Gateway. On restart, connected_event -> setup_subscriptions only does reqAllOpenOrdersAsync to feed the book (line 458-460); there is no pass that detects and cancels orphaned staged legs (e.g. by orderRef='proposal'), no persisted record that a bracket was in flight, and the proposal is stuck in APPROVED. A staged parent can later be transmitted (TWS UI, or a subsequent transmit on the same parent chain) producing an entry with no stop, and _place_and_wait's `await event.wait()` (line 962) has no timeout so a wedged leg also hangs the RPC handler indefinitely.

- **Failure scenario:** trader_service is killed by the 24GB container limit during a DuckDB checkpoint just after staging entry+TP for a 500-share bracket. Service restarts, book shows two inactive staged orders nobody owns. Days later an operator poking at TWS transmits the pending parent: 500 shares fill with no stop-loss and the proposal record still says APPROVED with no order_ids.

- **Suggested fix:** On connected_event, sweep open orders for untransmitted/parent-less legs with orderRef='proposal' and cancel them; persist an execution journal (proposal_id -> staged order ids) before placing leg 1; add a timeout to _place_and_wait.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: place_expressive_order stages entry with transmit=False (line 980) and _place_and_wait's `await event.wait()` (line 962) has no timeout. Restart only does reqAllOpenOrdersAsync, no orphan-leg sweep. For a live-money system this is a genuine capital-safety gap: a crash between legs (realistic given the documented 24GB OOM/DuckDB-checkpoint risk) can leave a staged parent that, if later transmitted, produces a filled entry with no stop-loss. The missing RPC timeout can also wedge the handler. Material for this system, not enterprise hand-wringing.


### [x] MEDIUM — Single global _mmr_lock serializes all SDK calls; a slow trader_service stalls the whole dashboard

- **Location:** `web/app.py:97`  ·  _reviewer: web_

- **Mechanism:** _call acquires the process-wide _mmr_lock around every SDK RPC. A single GET / issues 8 fetchers sequentially (cash, snapshot, status, risk, risk_limits, positions, strategies, proposals), each a separate _call holding the lock, each with the SDK's 30s RPC timeout. The approve/reject handlers also go through _call and the same lock. A trader_service that is slow-but-not-failing (does not raise ConnectionError/TimeoutError quickly) makes one page load hold the lock for up to ~8×30s, and because the lock is global, concurrent requests — including approve/reject and the 15s auto-refresh from every open tab — queue behind it. The retry-on-drop logic only helps on hard ConnectionError/TimeoutError, not on a hung-but-responsive service.

- **Failure scenario:** IB gateway degrades and trader_service RPCs take ~25s each. The dashboard page load holds _mmr_lock for minutes; meanwhile the operator clicks Reject on a bad pending proposal, but that POST blocks on the same lock and does not take effect until the slow page fetch finishes — the operator cannot act on trades when they most need to.

- **Suggested fix:** Give approve/reject their own connection/lock separate from read fetchers, shorten read-path RPC timeouts, and fetch dashboard sections concurrently (or make the read path not block the mutation path) so a slow read never gates a trade action.

- **Materiality:** Verified: _call wraps every SDK RPC in a single process-wide _mmr_lock, GET / issues 8 sequential fetchers, and approve/reject share the same lock. A slow-but-responsive trader_service (which won't hit the fast ConnectionError/TimeoutError retry) can make a page load hold the lock for up to ~8x30s, queuing the very reject/approve the operator needs during a degradation. Genuine operational fragility exactly when it matters. Lower probability at single-user scale (needs the hung-not-failing state), but the fix — separate mutation path/lock and shorter read timeouts — is sound.


### [ ] LOW — Internal _position_open flag desyncs from real position across restarts, orphaning exits

- **Location:** `strategies/regime_adaptive.py:87`  ·  _reviewer: probe-1:Strategy-file lookahead leaks and on_bar_

- **Mechanism:** RegimeAdaptive tracks its own position with `self._position_open` (line 87), set True on BUY and False on the regime/EMA/BB exit SELLs. This boolean is entirely internal and never reconciled against the actual portfolio. On a strategy_service restart the instance is reconstructed with `_position_open = False`, so if a real long position remains open, the strategy will never satisfy the `and self._position_open` conditions (lines 148, 186) and thus never publishes its exit SELL signal for that position; conversely it can re-issue a BUY on a fresh crossover while already effectively long. It also cannot see fills that were rejected/partial downstream.

- **Failure scenario:** Strategy enters a long (publishes BUY, sets _position_open=True). strategy_service is restarted (deploy, crash, pycron cycle). A bearish EMA crossover later occurs, but because _position_open was reset to False the exit-SELL branch is skipped, so the regime-exit signal for the still-open position is never emitted, leaving the position without its intended reversal-based exit.

- **Suggested fix:** Reconcile position state from the actual portfolio/universe on install/enable rather than a process-local boolean, or make exits unconditional on the internal flag and let the downstream consumer enforce position semantics.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: regime_adaptive tracks _position_open as a process-local bool (line 87), reset to False on __init__, never reconciled against the portfolio. On a strategy_service restart a still-open long loses its regime/EMA/BB exit signal. Real correctness bug affecting live exits, but confined to one example strategy (user code) and only produces a missed advisory signal, so low severity.


### [ ] LOW — Dead legacy packages (trader/batch, trader/cli, trader/portfolio) remain shipped and importable — one imports a dependency that no longer exists

- **Location:** `trader/cli/commands.py:14`  ·  _reviewer: architecture_

- **Mechanism:** trader/batch/* imports `arctic` (removed from dependencies) and rq-style queuers from the pre-DuckDB era; trader/cli/commands.py (48KB) imports trader.batch and so ImportErrors on load; trader/portfolio (life_simulator, vector_life, quantum_harmonic) are unrelated Monte-Carlo life simulators that mutate sys.path at import time. None are referenced by the live entry points (mmr_cli.py, services, pyproject scripts), but they are packaged, importable, and superficially look like the real CLI/history pipeline. In a codebase explicitly maintained by LLM iteration this is an active hazard: an agent grepping for 'history queuer' or 'cli commands' can plausibly wire new code to the dead path (the 10k-line mmr_cli.py next to a legacy trader/cli package invites exactly that confusion).

- **Failure scenario:** A future change (human or LLM) imports trader.cli or extends trader/batch believing it is the live CLI/data path; it crashes on the missing `arctic` module at best, or at worst resurrects the legacy Queuer write path against the current DuckDB schema.

- **Suggested fix:** Delete trader/batch, trader/cli, and trader/portfolio (git history preserves them), or move them to an attic/ directory excluded from the package; consider splitting mmr_cli.py by command group while at it.

- **Materiality:** Verified: trader/batch, trader/cli, trader/portfolio all still ship, and trader/cli/commands.py + trader/batch/*.py import `arctic` which is no longer a dependency, so they ImportError on load. None are on the live entry-point paths, so there is no runtime risk to the trading system — this is purely code hygiene. But given CLAUDE.md explicitly frames the codebase as LLM-iterated, a 10k-line mmr_cli.py sitting next to a dead trader/cli package and a legacy Queuer write path is a real trap for a future agent grepping for 'cli commands' or 'history queuer'. Low severity, cheap to fix by deleting or moving to an attic/ excluded from packaging.


### [ ] LOW — Ticker PubSub has no delivery guarantee, HWM tuning, or gap detection while strategies build persistent bars from it

- **Location:** `trader/messaging/clientserver.py:895`  ·  _reviewer: messaging_

- **Mechanism:** MultithreadedTopicPubSub uses raw PUB/SUB with default HWMs (1000) and no sequence numbers. PUB silently drops messages when a subscriber's queue fills (strategy_service blocked on a DuckDB write or GC pause), and the slow-joiner window plus any strategy_service restart loses all ticks in between. strategy_runtime builds bar DataFrames from this stream, persists them via tick_data.write(), and feeds indicators/signals from them, with no gap detection or reconciliation against history for the live window — so silent tick loss becomes silently wrong bars becomes signals computed on incomplete data (stale-data trading). Acceptable for display, but the strategy path treats the stream as if it were reliable.

- **Failure scenario:** strategy_service stalls ~10s on a DuckDB checkpoint during a fast market; the SUB queue overflows and PUB drops ticks. The 1-min bar for that window is built from a partial set of ticks (wrong high/low/volume), the SMI crossover fires on the distorted bar, and a signal is emitted/persisted that would not exist on true data.

- **Suggested fix:** Add a monotonically increasing sequence number per topic to published frames and have subscribers log/flag gaps; on detected gaps (or reconnect), backfill the affected window from trader_service history before letting strategies act on it.

- **Materiality:** Verified the design: raw PUB/SUB, no sequence numbers, strategies build persistent bars off the stream. The silent-wrong-bars mechanism is real and matches the project's silent-wrong-data concern. But at this actual scale the trigger is unlikely: minutes-cadence single subscriber, default HWM 1000 means the SUB queue would need to be stalled long enough to buffer 1000 undelivered ticks before PUB drops any — a 10s checkpoint pause won't get there at seconds-level tick rates. The more realistic loss (restart/slow-joiner window) is largely re-covered by the history-backed data refresh on startup. Worth a lightweight gap counter, but full per-topic sequencing + reconciliation is the most enterprise-flavored ask in this batch. Keep as a low-priority hardening item.


### [ ] LOW — Sweep symbol resolution takes the first match across all universes with no exchange/secType constraint

- **Location:** `trader/mmr_cli.py:5484`  ·  _reviewer: cli-2_

- **Mechanism:** _resolve_sym_to_conid calls accessor.resolve_universe(symbol, first_only=True) with no exchange, universe, or sec_type filter, and _expand_sweep_jobs binds the sweep job to whatever conId comes back first in universe iteration order ('portfolio' first, then sorted names — see universe.py list_universes). For dual-listed tickers present in multiple universes (e.g. BHP as both the ASX listing and the NYSE ADR), the pick is arbitrary. Worse, resolve_universe's numeric-string branch (universe.py line ~164) matches a purely numeric ticker string against conIds before symbol names, so an HK-style ticker like '0700' is first tried as conId 700 — exactly the string/conId coercion pattern the Design Principles call out as a bug, in the opposite direction. All persisted runs, the digest, and the leaderboard then carry the wrong instrument with no error.

- **Failure scenario:** A manifest lists `symbols: [BHP]` intending the ASX listing; the 'portfolio' universe contains the US ADR, which wins first_only resolution. 90 backtest runs are executed and persisted against the ADR's conid, the digest shows strong results, and the strategy is deployed with `--conids <ADR>` — trading a different instrument (exchange, currency, session hours) than the one the strategy was designed for.

- **Suggested fix:** Require an explicit exchange qualifier (or the SYMBOL.EXCHANGE form) when a symbol resolves in more than one universe/definition — fail loudly on ambiguity per the 'precision over convenience' principle — and never coerce numeric ticker strings to conId lookups on the symbol path.

- **Materiality:** Verified: _resolve_sym_to_conid uses resolve_universe(first_only=True) with no exchange/secType filter, and universe.py has the numeric-string->conId branch that fires before symbol matching. This is a genuine silent-wrong-instrument path and directly contradicts the codebase's own 'precision over convenience / no int->string coercion' principle. But it's narrow at this scale: the operator writes their own manifests, this US-centric system rarely touches dual-listed or numeric (HK-style) tickers, the digest shows the conid on review, and deployment uses explicit --conids. Real sharp edge, low blast radius. Worth a fail-on-ambiguity guard but not urgent.


### [ ] LOW — Sweep digest 'Strong candidates' section applies no minimum trade count

- **Location:** `trader/mmr_cli.py:6039`  ·  _reviewer: cli-2_

- **Mechanism:** _write_sweep_digest's `strong` filter selects runs with sharpe_ratio > 2.0 and profit_factor > 1.5 (or 'inf'), with no reliability gate. This is inconsistent with the rest of the codebase, which deliberately penalizes small samples (_bt_composite_score multiplies by 0.2 below 10 trades; _bt_row_is_strong requires >= 30 trades). A 2-trade run with two lucky winners has pf='inf' and often sharpe > 2, so it headlines the 'Strong candidates' table in the morning digest — the artifact the workflow tells the user/LLM to read first (`cat ~/.local/share/mmr/reports/sweep_*.md`).

- **Failure scenario:** A nightly sweep produces a 3-trade, all-winner grid point (sharpe 4.1, pf inf) that ranks as the #1 'Strong candidate' in the digest above a 250-trade sharpe-2.2 run; the morning review deploys the noise fit to paper/live.

- **Suggested fix:** Gate the strong-candidates list on total_trades >= 30 (mirroring _bt_row_is_strong), or annotate sub-threshold rows as 'insufficient sample'.

- **Materiality:** Verified: the 'strong' filter gates only on sharpe>2 and pf inf/>1.5 with no trade-count floor, while the rest of the codebase (_bt_composite_score, _bt_row_is_strong) deliberately penalizes small samples. A 2-3 trade all-winner run can headline the morning digest the workflow tells you to read first. Material because it can bias a deploy decision on a live-money system, but bounded: the documented deploy gate is the statistical-confidence block (backtests confidence), and the digest is explicitly 'not a decision metric.' Cheap one-line fix (gate on total_trades>=30).


### [ ] LOW — --news-bodies enrichment attaches the top Google News hit for '{ticker} stock news' with no relevance validation

- **Location:** `trader/mmr_cli.py:9113`  ·  _reviewer: idea-scanner_

- **Mechanism:** The news-bodies enrichment in _handle_ideas free-text searches Google News for f'{ticker} stock news', takes the first result unconditionally, scrapes it, and attaches a 400-char excerpt as news_body/news_url/news_title on that scan row. There is no check that the article is about the company (short/ambiguous tickers like A, ALL, IT, KEY, ON, NOW routinely match unrelated articles), and unlike the Polygon path there is no ticker-insight linkage. In JSON mode this lands directly in LLM context as if it were the candidate's news.

- **Failure scenario:** Scan surfaces ticker 'ON' (ON Semiconductor); the Google News top hit for 'ON stock news' is a generic market wrap or an article about a different company; its excerpt is attached to the ON row and the LLM cites it as an ON-specific catalyst in a proposal's reasoning.

- **Suggested fix:** Search with the resolved company name (already available via --detail names) plus ticker, and require the ticker or company name to appear in the scraped article before attaching; otherwise leave the fields empty.

- **Materiality:** Real data-quality bug: unconditional top-Google-News-hit for short/ambiguous tickers (ON, ALL, IT, A) can attach unrelated article text into LLM context as candidate news. Same class as 33 (silent wrong data feeding LLM reasoning) but distinct code path. Advisory/reviewed, so low severity, but a legitimate correctness fix for a single-operator LLM loop.


### [ ] LOW — Backtester accepts risk_limits/RiskGate but never enforces them — half-migrated dead parameter, backtest/live divergence

- **Location:** `trader/simulation/backtester.py:156`  ·  _reviewer: backtester_

- **Mechanism:** `Backtester.__init__` takes `risk_limits` and imports RiskGate/RiskLimits, but `self.risk_limits` is never referenced anywhere in `run()` or elsewhere in the file. Live trading enforces pre-trade limits (max position size, signal rate, daily loss) via the risk gate before every order; the backtest fills every affordable signal unconditionally. A strategy whose backtest edge depends on trades or sizes the live risk gate would block shows inflated simulated performance, and the constructor parameter gives a false impression that limits were simulated.

- **Failure scenario:** A high-frequency-signal strategy backtests to +20% with 400 trades; deployed live, the risk gate's signal-rate and max-position-size limits reject half its orders, and realized performance bears no relation to the backtest that justified deployment.

- **Suggested fix:** Either wire a RiskGate check into `_execute_signal` (rejecting/logging blocked fills so the divergence is measurable) or remove the parameter and document that backtests are ungated.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: self.risk_limits is assigned at backtester.py:156 and referenced nowhere else — a dead constructor param that falsely implies limits were simulated. The backtest/live divergence is real (live enforces signal-rate/max-position via the risk gate; backtest fills unconditionally), but for a dozens-of-positions, minutes-cadence single operator the odds a strategy's backtested edge hinges on trades the gate would block are low, and the operator reviews each order via propose/approve anyway. Worth a cleanup (wire it in or delete the param), but low materiality.


### [ ] LOW — Massive/TwelveData indicator enrichment degrades silently with no all-failed guard (unlike the IB path)

- **Location:** `trader/tools/idea_scanner.py:728`  ·  _reviewer: idea-scanner_

- **Mechanism:** IdeaScanner._fetch_indicators (line 727-729) and TwelveDataIdeaScanner._fetch_indicators (line 1765-1767) swallow every fetch exception at logger.debug and return None values. The IB path was hardened to raise IdeaScannerError when indicator inputs are entirely missing (line 1138), but the US paths — including the default TwelveData path per default_data_source — have no equivalent: if the API key loses its indicators entitlement, the endpoint 429s, or the time_series call breaks, every candidate is scored with rsi/ema/sma == None and the scan returns confident-looking rankings computed from price/volume terms only, with zero indication that half the scoring inputs were absent.

- **Failure scenario:** TwelveData rate-limits all time_series calls mid-scan (60 calls in a burst against a per-minute credit budget); every RSI/SMA is None, mean-reversion scores collapse to distance-below-SMA=0 + rel_vol only, and the LLM receives a ranked BUY list whose ordering is essentially noise, with no warning in --json output.

- **Suggested fix:** Mirror the IB path: track per-ticker indicator failures and raise (or emit a prominent warning field in the returned data) when the failure ratio exceeds a threshold.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: both Massive and TwelveData _fetch_indicators swallow exceptions at logger.debug and return None (line 727-729), while the IB path raises IdeaScannerError. This directly violates the codebase's stated 'fail loudly / no silent-wrong-data' principle, and the default_data_source is twelvedata, so it's the hot path. Impact is bounded — the scan is advisory and proposals are reviewed before approval — but silently ranking on noise with no signal in --json output is a real inconsistency worth flagging at low severity.


### [ ] LOW — Group budget computed from unnormalized (multi-currency) gross market values over base-currency net-liq

- **Location:** `trader/trading/portfolio_risk.py:152`  ·  _reviewer: probe-2:position_groups store and group-budget e_

- **Mechanism:** group_value sums p['value'] (portfolio_risk.py:152), which is abs(marketValue) taken straight from IB portfolio items (sdk.py:487 mkt_value) without FX normalization, then divides by net_liquidation drawn from a single NetLiquidation account value (sdk.py:888). For a portfolio holding international names (ASX in AUD, TSE in JPY) alongside USD names, the numerator mixes currency units while the denominator is the base currency, so group_pct is systematically wrong for multi-currency groups. Using abs() also counts short positions toward a long-allocation budget, which may misrepresent 'allocation' for hedged groups. This mirrors the codebase's systemic missing-FX handling and directly affects the over_budget decision.

- **Failure scenario:** A 'mining' group holds BHP on ASX (marketValue reported ~150,000 AUD) in a USD-base account. group_value treats 150,000 AUD as 150,000 USD, inflating group_pct by the AUD/USD rate and spuriously tripping (or, in the reverse direction for JPY, hiding) an over-budget warning.

- **Suggested fix:** Normalize each position's market value to the account base currency (using IB FX rates) before summing for group budgets, consistent with net_liquidation's currency; document whether budgets are gross or net.

- **Materiality:** Confirmed: group_value sums p['value'] (abs marketValue, unnormalized) over base-currency net_liquidation (line 152-154). This operator actively trades ASX (AUD) and TSE (JPY) alongside USD, so multi-currency group budgets are systematically mis-scaled by the FX rate, and abs() lumps shorts into a long-allocation budget. Real silent-wrong-data issue affecting the over_budget decision. Advisory soft-limit and only bites mixed-currency groups, so low severity. Distinct from 38 (this is the calculation, that is the exception handling), not a duplicate.


## Medium bugs


### [x] MEDIUM — Restart-suppression window uses timedelta.seconds and grows unboundedly with start_count

- **Location:** `pycron/pycron.py:294`  ·  _reviewer: ops_

- **Mechanism:** recently_started_with_error gates restart_if_finished with '(dt.datetime.now() - job.last_started).seconds < self.polling_period * (job.start_count + 1)'. Two defects: (1) '.seconds' is the day-wrapped component (max 86399), not total_seconds(), so elapsed time is effectively measured modulo 24h — a job whose last error exit was 3 days + 10s ago reads as '10 seconds ago'. (2) The window is polling_period*(start_count+1) where start_count increments on EVERY process exit for the life of pycron and never resets on successful runs — after N lifetime exits (e.g. from IB Gateway's nightly AUTO_RESTART_TIME bouncing trader_service), a single rc>0 exit suppresses restart for 60*(N+1) seconds, and once N≥1439 the window exceeds the 86399 ceiling of '.seconds', making the condition permanently true: the job is never restarted again until pycron itself restarts.

- **Failure scenario:** In the non-Docker pycron deployment, trader_service has accumulated 60 exits over two months of nightly gateway restarts. One morning it exits rc=1 during the IB re-auth window. pycron then refuses to restart it for 61 minutes of market open — or, at higher start_count, forever — while positions run unmanaged.

- **Suggested fix:** Use .total_seconds(), cap the backoff window (e.g. max 5 minutes), and reset start_count (or track consecutive failures separately) after a successful run of reasonable duration.

- **Verifier:** Traced end-to-end in actual code. pycron.py:294 uses timedelta.seconds (0–86399, day-wrapped) not total_seconds(); last_started is set at start (line 188) never at finish, so a long-lived service that crashes reads as "just started." start_count is init 0 (line 39), incremented on every exit (line 203), and never reset — window = 60·(start_count+1) grows without bound; once start_count≥1439 the window exceeds .seconds' 86399 ceiling and restart is permanently suppressed while return_code>0. The gate governs trader_service/strategy_service (restart_if_finished:True, no crontab_stop) via the restart branch at pycron.py:366–374, per config_defaults/pycron.yaml:28,37,48. So an rc=1 exit delays restart of live-position-managing services by minutes-to-an-hour, or forever at high counts. Severity medium: capital-safety impact is real (unmanaged positions), but the permanent-lockout case needs ~1439 lifetime exits; the common harm is bounded restart delay.


### [ ] MEDIUM — "ATR_day" gate is actually a per-bar true-range mean, neutering the notable-drive filter

- **Location:** `strategies/opening_drive_fade.py:78`  ·  _reviewer: probe-1:Strategy-file lookahead leaks and on_bar_

- **Mechanism:** Line 77-78 computes `atr = tr.rolling(14*390, min_periods=390).mean()` where `tr` is the per-BAR (1-minute) true range. The code and docstring call this ATR(14-day) / ATR_day and compare a full-session drive magnitude `drive_down = day_open - drive_low` (dollars over 30 minutes) against `atr * DRIVE_ATR_MULT` (line 131) and the near-low tolerance `low - drive_low > 0.2*atr` (line 145). But a rolling MEAN of 1-min true ranges is the average size of a single minute bar (cents), not a daily range (dollars). So the 1.5x-ATR 'must be a notable move' gate the docstring says gates trades 'hard' is satisfied by essentially any downward drift (e.g. a $0.10 30-min drop trivially exceeds 1.5 x ~$0.03), while the 0.2*atr near-low gate becomes absurdly tight (within a couple cents). The core risk filter is miscalibrated by roughly two orders of magnitude.

- **Failure scenario:** Backtest/live on 1-min AAPL: a mild 30-min drift of $0.15 below the open with RSI 29 and a low-volume bar below VWAP passes `drive_down (0.15) >= 1.5 * atr (~0.045)` even though this is not a '1.5 ATR_day' move at all. The strategy fades ordinary non-events instead of true opening drives, so both the backtest edge and live signals reflect a strategy fundamentally different from the documented thesis.

- **Suggested fix:** Compute a genuine daily ATR: derive per-session true range (session high-low, gap vs prior close) resampled to one value per day, take a 14-day rolling mean, then broadcast back to bars; or explicitly scale by the average daily range. Do not average 1-min true ranges and label it ATR_day.

- **Verifier:** Confirmed at strategies/opening_drive_fade.py:73-78: `tr` is a per-1-min-bar true range and `atr = tr.rolling(14*390).mean()` is the mean per-BAR range (cents), not a daily ATR (dollars) — `.mean()` divides by the window. So line 131's `drive_down (30-min move, dollars) < atr*1.5` gate is ~1.5-2 orders of magnitude too loose, and line 145's `0.2*atr` near-low tolerance is absurdly tight. The core "1.5 ATR_day" thesis (docstring, line 3-7; comment line 72) is not what the code computes. One nuance: the report's "fades ordinary non-events" overstates it — the too-tight near-low gate (145) partly counteracts, but the mislabeled ATR is real. Severity is medium not high: the strategy is not in strategy_runtime.yaml (only in sweeps/*.yaml backtest manifests), so there is no live order path — the concrete harm is a mis-measured backtest, not immediate capital loss.
